</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_power">Nuclear power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Nuclear power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_renaissance" title="Nuclear renaissance">Nuclear renaissance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg/200px-Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg" width="200" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg/300px-Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg/400px-Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2791" data-file-height="3668" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Blue <a href="/wiki/Cherenkov_radiation" title="Cherenkov radiation">Cherenkov</a> light being produced near the core of the Fission powered <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Test_Reactor" title="Advanced Test Reactor">Advanced Test Reactor</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since about 2001 the term "nuclear renaissance" has been used to refer to a possible <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> industry revival, driven by rising <a href="/wiki/Price_of_petroleum" class="mw-redirect" title="Price of petroleum">fossil fuel prices</a> and new concerns about meeting <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emission limits.<sup id="cite_ref-intro_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-intro-78">[78]</a></sup> However, in March 2011 the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_nuclear_disaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Fukushima nuclear disaster">Fukushima nuclear disaster</a> in Japan and associated shutdowns at <a href="/wiki/2011_Japanese_nuclear_incidents" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Japanese nuclear incidents">other nuclear facilities</a> raised questions among some commentators over the future of nuclear power.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Platts" class="mw-redirect" title="Platts">Platts</a> has reported that "the crisis at Japan's Fukushima nuclear plants has prompted leading energy-consuming countries to review the safety of their existing reactors and cast doubt on the speed and scale of planned expansions around the world".<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Nuclear_Association" title="World Nuclear Association">World Nuclear Association</a> has reported that nuclear electricity generation in 2012 was at its lowest level since 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-wna13_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wna13-83">[83]</a></sup> Several previous international studies and assessments,<sup id="cite_ref-Sims.2C_R.E.H..2C_et_al._84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sims.2C_R.E.H..2C_et_al.-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IAEA_2008_42_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA_2008_42-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> suggested that as part of the portfolio of other <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Low-carbon energy">low-carbon energy</a> technologies, nuclear power will continue to play a role in reducing greenhouse gas emissions. Historically, nuclear power usage is estimated to have prevented the atmospheric emission of 64 giga<a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">CO2-equivalent</a> as of 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Public concerns about nuclear power include the <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">fate of spent nuclear fuel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_accidents" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear accidents">nuclear accidents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">security risks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants">a concern that nuclear power plants are very expensive</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bm_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bm-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Of these concerns, nuclear accidents and disposal of long-lived radioactive fuel/"waste" have probably had the greatest public impact worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-bm_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bm-88">[88]</a></sup> Although generally unaware of it, both of these glaring public concerns are greatly diminished by present <a href="/wiki/Passive_safety" class="mw-redirect" title="Passive safety">passive safety</a> designs, the experimentally proven, "melt-down proof" <a href="/wiki/EBR-II" class="mw-redirect" title="EBR-II">EBR-II</a>, future <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_reactor" title="Molten salt reactor">molten salt reactors</a>, and the use of conventional and more advanced fuel/"waste" <a href="/wiki/Pyroprocessing" title="Pyroprocessing">pyroprocessing</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> with the latter recycling or <a href="/wiki/Reprocessing" class="mw-disambig" title="Reprocessing">reprocessing</a><sup class="plainlinks noprint Inline-Template" style="vertical-align:text-top;white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://dispenser.homenet.org/~dispenser/cgi-bin/dab_solver.py?page=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;editintro=Template:Disambiguation_needed/editintro&amp;client=Template:Dn"><span title="Link needs disambiguation. (January 2017)">disambiguation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> not presently being commonplace as it is often considered to be cheaper to use a once-through <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">nuclear fuel cycle</a> in many countries, depending on the varying levels of intrinsic value given by a society in reducing the long-lived waste in their country, with France doing a considerable amount of reprocessing when compared to the US.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">Nuclear power</a>, with a 10.6% share of world electricity production as of 2013, is second only to hydroelectricity as the largest source of low-carbon power.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Over 400 reactors generate electricity in 31 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> review published in the Journal of Industrial Ecology analyzing CO<sub>2</sub> <a href="/wiki/Life_cycle_assessment" class="mw-redirect" title="Life cycle assessment">life cycle assessment</a>(LCA) emissions from nuclear power(<a href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Light water reactor">Light water reactors</a>) determined that: "The collective LCA literature indicates that life cycle <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">GHG</a> emissions from nuclear power are only a fraction of traditional fossil sources and comparable to renewable technologies."<sup id="cite_ref-Warner_.2B_Heath.2C_JoIE_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warner_.2B_Heath.2C_JoIE-96">[96]</a></sup> While some have raised uncertainty surrounding the future GHG emissions of nuclear power as a result of an extreme potential decline in uranium ore grade without a corresponding increase in the <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">efficiency of enrichment methods</a>. In a scenario analysis of future global nuclear development, as it could be effected by a decreasing global <a href="/wiki/Uranium_market" title="Uranium market">uranium market</a> of average ore grade, the analysis determined that depending on conditions, median life cycle nuclear power GHG emissions could be between 9 and 110&#160;g CO<sub>2</sub>-eq/kWh by 2050, with the latter high figure being derived from a "worst-case scenario" that is not "considered very robust" by the authors of the paper, as the "ore grade" in the scenario is lower than the uranium concentration in many lignite coal ashes.<sup id="cite_ref-Warner_.2B_Heath.2C_JoIE_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warner_.2B_Heath.2C_JoIE-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although this future analyses primarily deals with extrapolations for present <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">Generation II reactor</a> technology, the same paper also summarizes the literature on "FBRs"/<a href="/wiki/Fast_Breeder_Reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Fast Breeder Reactor">Fast Breeder Reactors</a>, of which two are in operation as of 2014 with the newest being the <a href="/wiki/BN-800_reactor" title="BN-800 reactor">BN-800</a>, for these reactors it states that the "median life cycle GHG emissions&#160;... [are] similar to or lower than [present light water reactors] <a href="/wiki/LWR" class="mw-redirect" title="LWR">LWRs</a> and purports to consume little or no uranium ore.<i><sup id="cite_ref-Warner_.2B_Heath.2C_JoIE_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warner_.2B_Heath.2C_JoIE-96">[96]</a></sup></i></p>
<p>In their 2014 report, the IPCC <a href="/wiki/Comparisons_of_life-cycle_greenhouse_gas_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="Comparisons of life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions">comparison of energy sources global warming potential per unit of electricity generated</a>, which notably included <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> effects, mirror the <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">median</a> emission value derived from the Warner and Heath Yale <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> for the more common non-breeding <a href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Light water reactor">Light water reactors</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">CO2-equivalent</a> value of 12&#160;g CO2-eq/kWh, which is the lowest global warming forcing of all <a href="/wiki/Baseload" class="mw-redirect" title="Baseload">baseload</a> power sources, with comparable low carbon power baseload sources, such as hydropower and biomass, producing substantially more global warming forcing 24 and 230&#160;g CO2-eq/kWh respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_2014_Annex_III_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2014_Annex_III-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a> published <i>The Net Benefits of Low and No-Carbon Electricity Technologies</i> which states, after performing an energy and emissions cost analysis, that "The net benefits of new nuclear, hydro, and natural gas combined cycle plants far outweigh the net benefits of new wind or solar plants", with the most cost effective low carbon power technology being determined to be nuclear power.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>During his <a href="/wiki/President_of_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="President of United States">presidential</a> campaign, <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> stated, "Nuclear power represents more than 70% of our noncarbon generated electricity. It is unlikely that we can meet our aggressive climate goals if we eliminate nuclear power as an option."<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cc/Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg/220px-Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cc/Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg/330px-Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cc/Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg/440px-Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="758" data-file-height="399" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This graph illustrates nuclear power is the United States's largest contributor of non-greenhouse-gas-emitting electric power generation, comprising nearly three-quarters of the non-emitting sources.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Analysis in 2015 by Professor and Chair of Environmental Sustainability <a href="/wiki/Barry_Brook_(scientist)" title="Barry Brook (scientist)">Barry W. Brook</a> and his colleagues on the topic of replacing fossil fuels entirely, from the electric grid of the world, has determined that at the historically modest and proven-rate at which nuclear energy was added to and replaced fossil fuels in France and Sweden during each nation's building programs in the 1980s, within 10 years nuclear energy could displace or remove fossil fuels from the electric grid completely, "allow[ing] the world to meet the most stringent greenhouse-gas mitigation targets.".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> In a similar analysis, Brook had earlier determined that 50% of all <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">global energy</a>, that is not solely electricity, but transportation <a href="/wiki/Synfuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Synfuels">synfuels</a> etc. could be generated within approximately 30 years, if the global nuclear fission build rate was identical to each of these nation's already proven decadal rates(in units of installed <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">GW</a> per year, per unit of global <a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a>(GW/year/$).<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qualenergia.it_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qualenergia.it-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>This is in contrast to the completely conceptual paper-studies for a <i><a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a></i> world, which would require an <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude" class="mw-redirect" title="Orders of magnitude">orders of magnitude</a> more costly global investment per year, an investment rate that has no historical precedent, having never been attempted due to its prohibitive cost,<sup id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and with far greater land area that would be required to be devoted to the wind, wave and solar projects, along with the inherent assumption that humanity will use less, and not more, energy in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qualenergia.it_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qualenergia.it-105">[105]</a></sup> As Brook notes the "principal limitations on nuclear fission are not technical, economic or fuel-related, but are instead linked to complex issues of societal acceptance, fiscal and political inertia, and inadequate critical evaluation of the real-world constraints facing [the other] low-carbon alternatives."<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nuclear power may be uncompetitive compared with fossil fuel energy sources in countries without a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a> program, and in comparison to a fossil fuel plant of the same power output, nuclear power plants take a longer amount of time to construct.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tf2010_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tf2010-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iea2009_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea2009-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two new, first of their kind, <a href="/wiki/European_Pressurized_Reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="European Pressurized Reactor">EPR</a> reactors under construction in Finland and France have been delayed and are running over-budget.<sup id="cite_ref-jk_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jk-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-greeninc_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greeninc-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rb_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rb-113">[113]</a></sup> However learning from experience, two further EPR reactors under construction in China are on, and ahead, of schedule respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> As of 2013, according to the <a href="/wiki/IAEA" class="mw-redirect" title="IAEA">IAEA</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Nuclear_Society" title="European Nuclear Society">European Nuclear Society</a>, worldwide there were 68 civil nuclear power reactors under construction in 15 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-euronuclear.org_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euronuclear.org-116">[116]</a></sup> China has 29 of these nuclear power reactors under construction, as of 2013, with plans to build many more,<sup id="cite_ref-euronuclear.org_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euronuclear.org-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nuclear_Power_in_China_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear_Power_in_China-117">[117]</a></sup> while in the US the licenses of almost half its reactors have been extended to 60&#160;years,<sup id="cite_ref-Nuclear_Power_in_the_USA_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear_Power_in_the_USA-118">[118]</a></sup> and plans to build another dozen are under serious consideration.<sup id="cite_ref-matt2010_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matt2010-119">[119]</a></sup> There are also a considerable number of new reactors being built in South Korea, India, and Russia. At least 100 older and smaller reactors will "most probably be closed over the next 10–15 years".<sup id="cite_ref-ditt_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ditt-120">[120]</a></sup> This is probable only if one does not factor in the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Light_Water_Reactor_Sustainability_Program" title="Light Water Reactor Sustainability Program">Light Water Reactor Sustainability Program</a>, created to permit the extension of the life span of the USA's 104 nuclear reactors to 60 years. The licenses of almost half of the USA's reactors have been extended to 60&#160;years as of 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Nuclear_Power_in_the_USA_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear_Power_in_the_USA-118">[118]</a></sup> Two new "<a href="/wiki/Passive_safety" class="mw-redirect" title="Passive safety">passive safety</a>" <a href="/wiki/AP1000" title="AP1000">AP1000</a> reactors are, as of 2013, being constructed at <a href="/wiki/Vogtle_Electric_Generating_Plant" title="Vogtle Electric Generating Plant">Vogtle Electric Generating Plant</a>.</p>
<p>Public opinion about nuclear power varies widely between countries.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> A poll by Gallup International (2011)<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> assessed public opinion in 47 countries. The poll was conducted following a <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tōhoku earthquake and tsunami">tsunami and earthquake</a> which caused an <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">accident</a> at the Fukushima nuclear power plant in Japan. 49% stated that they held favourable views about nuclear energy, while 43% held an unfavourable view.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Another global survey by Ipsos (2011)<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> assessed public opinion in 24 countries. Respondents to this survey showed a clear preference for renewable energy sources over coal and nuclear energy (refer to graph opposite).<sup id="cite_ref-ipsos_2011_preference_for_renewables_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipsos_2011_preference_for_renewables-2">[2]</a></sup> Ipsos (2012)<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> found that solar and wind were viewed by the public as being more environmentally friendly and more viable long-term energy sources relative to nuclear power and natural gas. However, solar and wind were viewed as being less reliable relative to nuclear power and natural gas. In 2012 a poll done in the UK found that 63% of those surveyed support nuclear power, and with opposition to nuclear power at 11%.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> In Germany, strong <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-nuclear">anti-nuclear</a> sentiment led to eight of the seventeen operating reactors being permanently shut down following the March 2011 <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_nuclear_disaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Fukushima nuclear disaster">Fukushima nuclear disaster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Annika_Breidthardt_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Annika_Breidthardt-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">Nuclear fusion</a> research, in the form of the <a href="/wiki/International_Thermonuclear_Experimental_Reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="International Thermonuclear Experimental Reactor">International Thermonuclear Experimental Reactor</a> is underway. Fusion powered electricity generation was initially believed to be readily achievable, as fission power had been. However, the extreme requirements for continuous reactions and <a href="/wiki/Plasma_containment" title="Plasma containment">plasma containment</a> led to projections being extended by several decades. In 2010, more than 60 years after the first attempts, commercial power production was still believed to be unlikely before 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-ITERorg_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITERorg-129">[129]</a></sup> Although rather than an either, or, issue economical <a href="/wiki/Fusion-fission_hybrid_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Fusion-fission hybrid reactor">fusion-fission hybrid reactors</a> could be built before any attempt at this more demanding commercial "<a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">pure-fusion reactor</a>"/<a href="/wiki/DEMO" title="DEMO">DEMO</a> reactor takes place.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_to_gas_fuel_switching">Coal to gas fuel switching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Coal to gas fuel switching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">Emission intensity</a></div>
<p>Most mitigation proposals imply—rather than directly state—an eventual reduction in global fossil fuel production. Also proposed are direct quotas on global fossil fuel production.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Natural gas emits far fewer greenhouse gases (i.e. CO<sub>2</sub> and methane—CH<sub>4</sub>) than coal when burned at power plants, but evidence has been emerging that this benefit could be completely negated by methane leakage at gas drilling fields and other points in the supply chain.</p>
<p>A study performed by the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) and the Gas Research Institute (GRI) in 1997 sought to discover whether the reduction in carbon dioxide emissions from increased natural gas (predominantly methane) use would be offset by a possible increased level of methane emissions from sources such as leaks and emissions. The study concluded that the reduction in emissions from increased natural gas use outweighs the detrimental effects of increased methane emissions. More recent peer-reviewed studies have challenged the findings of this study, with researchers from the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) reconfirming findings of high rates of methane (CH4) leakage from natural gas fields.</p>
<p>A 2011 study<sup id="cite_ref-usclimatenetwork.org_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usclimatenetwork.org-133">[133]</a></sup> by noted climate research scientist, Tom Wigley,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> found that while carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>) emissions from fossil fuel combustion may be reduced by using natural gas rather than coal to produce energy, it also found that additional methane (CH4) from leakage adds to the radiative forcing of the climate system, offsetting the reduction in CO<sub>2</sub> forcing that accompanies the transition from coal to gas. The study looked at methane leakage from coal mining; changes in radiative forcing due to changes in the emissions of sulfur dioxide and carbonaceous aerosols; and differences in the efficiency of electricity production between coal- and gas-fired power generation. On balance, these factors more than offset the reduction in warming due to reduced CO<sub>2</sub> emissions. When gas replaces coal there is additional warming out to 2,050 with an assumed leakage rate of 0%, and out to 2,140 if the leakage rate is as high as 10%. The overall effects on global-mean temperature over the 21st century, however, are small. Petron et al. (2013)<sup id="cite_ref-epa.gov_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa.gov-135">[135]</a></sup> and Alvarez et al. (2012)<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> note that estimated that leakage from gas infrastructure is likely to be underestimated. These studies indicate that the exploitation of natural gas as a "cleaner" fuel is questionable. A 2014 meta-study of 20 years of natural gas technical literature shows that methane emissions are consistently underestimated but on a 100-year scale, the climate benefits of coal to gas fuel switching are likely larger than the negative effects of natural gas leakage.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heat_pump">Heat pump</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Heat pump">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heat_pump" title="Heat pump">heat pump</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg/220px-Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg/330px-Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg/440px-Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2112" data-file-height="2816" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Outside unit of an air-source heat pump.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A heat pump is a device that provides heat energy from a source of heat to a destination called a "heat sink". Heat pumps are designed to move <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> opposite to the direction of spontaneous heat flow by absorbing heat from a cold space and releasing it to a warmer one. A heat pump uses some amount of external power to accomplish the work of transferring energy from the heat source to the heat sink.</p>
<p>While <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioner" class="mw-redirect" title="Air conditioner">air conditioners</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freezer" class="mw-redirect" title="Freezer">freezers</a> are familiar examples of heat pumps, the term "heat pump" is more general and applies to many <a href="/wiki/HVAC" title="HVAC">HVAC</a> (heating, ventilating, and air conditioning) devices used for space heating or space cooling. When a heat pump is used for heating, it employs the same basic <a href="/wiki/Heat_pump_and_refrigeration_cycle" title="Heat pump and refrigeration cycle">refrigeration-type cycle</a> used by an air conditioner or a refrigerator, but in the opposite direction—releasing heat into the conditioned space rather than the surrounding environment. In this use, heat pumps generally draw heat from the cooler external air or from the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> In heating mode, heat pumps are three to four times <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">more efficient</a> in their use of electric power than simple electrical resistance heaters.</p>
<p>It has been concluded that heat pumps are the single technology that could reduce the greenhouse gas emissions of households better than every other technology that is available on the market. With a market share of 30% and (potentially) clean electricity, heat pumps could reduce global CO2 emissions by 8% annually.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Using ground source heat pumps could reduce around 60% of the <a href="/wiki/Primary_energy" title="Primary energy">primary energy</a> demand and 90% of CO2 emissions in Europe in 2050 and make handling high shares of renewable energy easier.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Using surplus renewable energy in heat pumps is regarded as the most effective household means to reduce global warming and fossil fuel depletion.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>With significant amounts of fossil fuel used in electricity production, demands on the electrical grid also generate greenhouse gases. Without a high share of low-carbon electricity, a domestic heat pump will produce more carbon emissions than using natural gas.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_fuel_phase-out:_carbon_neutral_and_negative_fuels">Fossil fuel phase-out: carbon neutral and negative fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Fossil fuel phase-out: carbon neutral and negative fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg/220px-Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg/330px-Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg/440px-Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1066" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
3,500–4,000 <a href="/wiki/Environmental_activist" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental activist">environmental activists</a> blocking a coal mine in Germany to limit climate change (<a href="/wiki/Ende_Gel%C3%A4nde_2016" title="Ende Gelände 2016">Ende Gelände 2016</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_phase-out" title="Fossil fuel phase-out">Fossil fuel phase-out</a></div>
<p>Fossil fuel may be phased-out with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_neutral_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon neutral fuel">carbon neutral and carbon negative</a> pipeline and transportation fuels created with <a href="/wiki/Power_to_gas" title="Power to gas">power to gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gas_to_liquids" title="Gas to liquids">gas to liquids</a> technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">Carbon dioxide</a> from fossil fuel <a href="/wiki/Flue_gas" title="Flue gas">flue gas</a> can be used to produce <a href="/wiki/Lumber#Plastic_lumber" title="Lumber">plastic lumber</a> allowing carbon negative <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">reforestation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demand_side_management">Demand side management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Demand side management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_efficiency_and_conservation">Energy efficiency and conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Energy efficiency and conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">Efficient energy use</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_conservation" title="Energy conservation">Energy conservation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg/220px-Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg" width="220" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg/330px-Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg/440px-Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="873" data-file-height="962" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A spiral-type integrated <a href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lamp" title="Compact fluorescent lamp">compact fluorescent lamp</a>, use has grown among North American consumers since its introduction in the mid-1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Efficient energy use, sometimes simply called "energy efficiency", is the goal of efforts to reduce the amount of energy required to provide products and services. For example, <a href="/wiki/Building_insulation" title="Building insulation">insulating a home</a> allows a building to use less heating and cooling energy to achieve and maintain a comfortable temperature. Installing <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lights" class="mw-redirect" title="Fluorescent lights">fluorescent lights</a> or natural <a href="/wiki/Skylight_(window)" class="mw-redirect" title="Skylight (window)">skylights</a> reduces the amount of energy required to attain the same level of illumination compared to using traditional <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulbs" class="mw-redirect" title="Incandescent light bulbs">incandescent light bulbs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lights" class="mw-redirect" title="Compact fluorescent lights">Compact fluorescent lights</a> use two-thirds less energy and may last 6 to 10 times longer than incandescent lights.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Energy efficiency has proved to be a cost-effective strategy for building economies without necessarily growing <a href="/wiki/World_energy_resources_and_consumption" class="mw-redirect" title="World energy resources and consumption">energy consumption</a>. For example, the state of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> began implementing energy-efficiency measures in the mid-1970s, including building code and appliance standards with strict efficiency requirements. During the following years, California's energy consumption has remained approximately flat on a per capita basis while national U.S. consumption doubled. As part of its strategy, California implemented a "loading order" for new energy resources that puts energy efficiency first, renewable electricity supplies second, and new fossil-fired power plants last.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Energy_conservation" title="Energy conservation">Energy conservation</a> is broader than energy efficiency in that it encompasses using less energy to achieve a lesser energy demanding service, for example through behavioral change, as well as encompassing energy efficiency. Examples of conservation without efficiency improvements would be heating a room less in winter, driving less, or working in a less brightly lit room. As with other definitions, the boundary between efficient energy use and energy conservation can be fuzzy, but both are important in environmental and economic terms. This is especially the case when actions are directed at the saving of fossil fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reducing energy use is seen as a key solution to the problem of reducing greenhouse gas emissions. According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a>, improved energy efficiency in <a href="/wiki/Energy-efficient_buildings" class="mw-redirect" title="Energy-efficient buildings">buildings</a>, industrial processes and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_transportation" class="mw-redirect" title="Sustainable transportation">transportation</a> could reduce the world's energy needs in 2050 by one third, and help control global emissions of greenhouse gases.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Demand_side_switching_sources">Demand side switching sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Demand side switching sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Fuel switching on the demand side refers to changing the type of fuel used to satisfy a need for an energy service. To meet deep decarbonization goals, like the 80% reduction by 2050 goal being discussed in California and the European Union, many primary energy changes are needed.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Energy efficiency alone may not be sufficient to meet these goals, switching fuels used on the demand side will help lower carbon emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> Progressively coal, oil and eventually natural gas for space and water heating in buildings will need to be reduced. For an equivalent amount of heat, burning natural gas produces about 45 per cent less carbon dioxide than burning <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gasdotorg_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gasdotorg-158">[158]</a></sup> There are various ways in which this could happen, and different strategies will likely make sense in different locations. While the system efficiency of a gas furnace may be higher than the combination of natural gas power plant and electric heat, the combination of the same natural gas power plant and an electric <a href="/wiki/Heat_pump" title="Heat pump">heat pump</a> has lower emissions per unit of heat delivered in all but the coldest climates. This is possible because of the very efficient <a href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_performance" title="Coefficient of performance">coefficient of performance</a> of heat pumps.</p>
<p>At the beginning of this century 70% of all electricity was generated by fossil fuels, and as carbon free sources eventually make up half of the generation mix, replacing gas or oil furnaces and water heaters with electric ones will have a climate benefit. In areas like Norway, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a> that have abundant hydroelectricity, electric heat and hot water is common.</p>
<p>The economics of switching the demand side from fossil fuels to electricity for heating, will depend on the price of fuels vs electricity and the relative prices of the equipment. The EIA Annual Energy Outlook 2014 suggests that domestic gas prices will rise faster than electricity prices which will encourage electrification in the coming decades.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Electrifying heating loads may also provide a flexible resource that can participate in <a href="/wiki/Demand_response" title="Demand response">demand response</a>. Since thermostatically controlled loads have inherent energy storage, electrification of heating could provide a valuable resource to integrate variable renewable resources into the grid.</p>
<p>Alternatives to electrification, include decarbonizing pipeline gas through <a href="/wiki/Power_to_gas" title="Power to gas">power to gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>, or other <a href="/wiki/Carbon_neutral_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon neutral fuels">carbon neutral fuels</a>. A 2015 study by Energy+Environmental Economics shows that a hybrid approach of decarbonizing pipeline gas, electrification, and energy efficiency can meet carbon reduction goals at a similar cost as only electrification and energy efficiency in Southern California.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Demand_side_grid_management">Demand side grid management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Demand side grid management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Expanding intermittent electrical sources such as <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, creates a growing problem balancing grid fluctuations. Some of the plans include building pumped storage or continental <a href="/wiki/Super_grid" title="Super grid">super grids</a> costing billions of dollars. However instead of building for more power,there are a variety of ways to affect the size and timing of electricity demand on the consumer side. Designing for reduced demands on a smaller power grid is more efficient and economic than having extra generation and transmission for intermittentcy, power failures and peak demands. Having these abilities is one of the chief aims of a <a href="/wiki/Smart_grid" title="Smart grid">smart grid</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Net_energy_metering#Time_of_use_metering" class="mw-redirect" title="Net energy metering">Time of use</a> metering is a common way to motivate electricity users to reduce their peak load consumption. For instance, running dishwashers and laundry at night after the peak has passed, reduces electricity costs.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dynamic_demand" class="mw-redirect" title="Dynamic demand">Dynamic demand</a> plans have devices passively shut off when stress is sensed on the electrical grid. This method may work very well with thermostats, when power on the grid sags a small amount, a low power temperature setting is automatically selected reducing the load on the grid. For instance millions of refrigerators reduce their consumption when clouds pass over solar installations. Consumers would need to have a <a href="/wiki/Smart_meter" title="Smart meter">smart meter</a> in order for the utility to calculate credits.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Demand_response" title="Demand response">Demand response</a> devices could receive all sorts of messages from the grid. The message could be a request to use a low power mode similar to dynamic demand, to shut off entirely during a sudden failure on the grid, or notifications about the current and expected prices for power. This would allow electric cars to recharge at the least expensive rates independent of the time of day. The <a href="/wiki/Vehicle-to-grid" title="Vehicle-to-grid">vehicle-to-grid</a> suggestion would use a car's battery or fuel cell to supply the grid temporarily.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifestyle_and_behavior">Lifestyle and behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Lifestyle and behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/IPCC" class="mw-redirect" title="IPCC">IPCC</a> <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fifth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fifth Assessment Report">Fifth Assessment Report</a> emphasises that behaviour, lifestyle and cultural change have a high mitigation potential in some sectors, particularly when complementing technological and structural change.<sup id="cite_ref-edenhofer-etal-2014_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edenhofer-etal-2014-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:20</sup> In general, higher consumption lifestyles have a greater environmental impact. Overall, food accounts for the largest share of consumption-based GHG emissions with nearly 20% of the global carbon footprint, followed by housing, mobility, services, manufactured products, and construction. Food and services are more significant in poor countries, while mobility and manufactured goods are more significant in rich countries.<sup id="cite_ref-fleurbaey-etal-2014_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fleurbaey-etal-2014-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:327</sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_change">Dietary change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dietary change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Low_carbon_diet" class="mw-redirect" title="Low carbon diet">Low carbon diet</a></div>
<p>A 2014 study into the real-life diets of British people estimates their greenhouse gas contributions (<a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">CO<sub>2</sub>eq</a>) to be: 7.19<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>kg/day for high meat-eaters through to 3.81<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>kg/day for vegetarians and 2.89<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>kg/day for vegans.<sup id="cite_ref-scarborough-etal-2014_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scarborough-etal-2014-163">[163]</a></sup> The widespread adoption of a vegetarian diet could cut food-related greenhouse gas emissions by 63% by 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-harvey-2016_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harvey-2016-164">[164]</a></sup> China introduced new dietary guidelines in 2016 which aim to cut meat consumption by 50% and thereby reduce greenhouse gas emissions by 1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion tonnes by 2030.<sup id="cite_ref-milman-2016_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-milman-2016-165">[165]</a></sup> A 2016 study concluded that taxes on meat and milk could simultaneously result in reduced greenhouse gas emissions and healthier diets. The study analyzed surcharges of 40% on beef and 20% on milk and suggests that an optimum plan would reduce emissions by 1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion tonnes per year.<sup id="cite_ref-carrington-2016_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carrington-2016-166">[166]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-springmann-etal-2016_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-springmann-etal-2016-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sinks_and_negative_emissions">Sinks and negative emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Sinks and negative emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">Carbon sink</a> and <a href="/wiki/Negative_carbon_dioxide_emission" title="Negative carbon dioxide emission">Negative carbon dioxide emission</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon sink</a> is a natural or artificial reservoir that accumulates and stores some carbon-containing chemical compound for an indefinite period, such as a growing <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forest</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Negative_carbon_dioxide_emission" title="Negative carbon dioxide emission">negative carbon dioxide emission</a> on the other hand is a permanent removal of carbon dioxide out of the atmosphere, such as directly capturing carbon dioxide in the atmosphere and storing it in <a href="/wiki/Geologic_sequestration_of_CO2" class="mw-redirect" title="Geologic sequestration of CO2">geologic formations</a> underground.</p>
<p>The Antarctic Climate and Ecosystems Cooperative Research Centre (ACE-CRC) notes that one third of humankind’s annual emissions of CO<sub>2</sub> are absorbed by the oceans.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> However, this also leads to <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>, with potentially significant impacts on marine life.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Acidification lowers the level of carbonate ions available for calcifying organisms to form their shells. These organisms include plankton species that contribute to the foundation of the Southern Ocean food web. However acidification may impact on a broad range of other physiological and ecological processes, such as fish respiration, larval development and changes in the solubility of both nutrients and toxins.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reforestation_and_afforestation">Reforestation and afforestation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Reforestation and afforestation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg/220px-Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg/330px-Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg/440px-Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2234" data-file-height="1490" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Transferring <a href="/wiki/Land_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Land rights">land rights</a> to indigenous inhabitants is argued to efficiently conserve forests. Regrowth of <a href="/wiki/Secondary_forest" title="Secondary forest">forests</a> on abandoned farmland restores more forest than that lost to <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">Reforestation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biosequestration" title="Biosequestration">Biosequestration</a></div>
<p>Almost 20 percent (8<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>GtCO<sub>2</sub>/year) of total greenhouse-gas emissions were from deforestation in 2007. It is estimated that avoided deforestation reduces CO<sub>2</sub> emissions at a rate of 1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>tonne of CO<sub>2</sub> per $1–5 in <a href="/wiki/Opportunity_costs" class="mw-redirect" title="Opportunity costs">opportunity costs</a> from lost agriculture. <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">Reforestation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afforestation" title="Afforestation">afforestation</a>, where there was previously no forest, could save at least another 1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>GtCO<sub>2</sub>/year, at an estimated cost of $5–15/tCO<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-stern_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stern-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>Transferring rights over land from public domain to its indigenous inhabitants is argued to be a cost effective strategy to conserve forests.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian1_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian1-172">[172]</a></sup> This includes the protection of such rights entitled in existing laws, such as India’s <a href="/wiki/The_Scheduled_Tribes_and_Other_Traditional_Forest_Dwellers_(Recognition_of_Forest_Rights)_Act,_2006" title="The Scheduled Tribes and Other Traditional Forest Dwellers (Recognition of Forest Rights) Act, 2006">Forest Rights Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian1_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian1-172">[172]</a></sup> The transferring of such rights in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, perhaps the largest <a href="/wiki/Land_reform" title="Land reform">land reform</a> in modern times, has been argued to have increased forest cover.<sup id="cite_ref-rightsandresources_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rightsandresources-173">[173]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, forested areas given tenure to indigenous groups have even lower rates of clearing than <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national parks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rightsandresources_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rightsandresources-173">[173]</a></sup> A 2016 report concludes that modest investments in indigenous land rights will generate economic, social, and environmental returns for the communities involved and for climate protection. The report quantifies the economic value of securing such rights, with a focus on the Amazon region.<sup id="cite_ref-ding-etal-2016-webpage_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ding-etal-2016-webpage-174">[174]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ding-etal-2016_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ding-etal-2016-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>With increased <a href="/wiki/Intensive_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Intensive agriculture">intensive agriculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a>, there is an increase in the amount of abandoned farmland. By some estimates, for every half a hectare of original <a href="/wiki/Old-growth_forest" title="Old-growth forest">old-growth forest</a> cut down, more than 20 hectares of new <a href="/wiki/Secondary_forest" title="Secondary forest">secondary forests</a> are growing, even though they do not have the same biodiversity as the original forests and original forests store 60% more carbon than these new secondary forests.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> According to a study in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>, promoting regrowth on abandoned farmland could offset years of carbon emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Avoided_desertification">Avoided desertification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Avoided desertification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cow_female_black_white.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/220px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/330px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/440px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2700" data-file-height="1803" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cow_female_black_white.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Managed grazing methods are argued to be able to restore grasslands, thereby significantly decreasing atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> levels.<sup id="cite_ref-ASavory_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ASavory-179">[179]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Restoring grasslands store CO<sub>2</sub> from the air into plant material. Grazing livestock, usually not left to wander, would eat the grass and would minimize any grass growth. However, grass left alone would eventually grow to cover its own growing buds, preventing them from photosynthesizing and the dying plant would stay in place.<sup id="cite_ref-Tlovell_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tlovell-180">[180]</a></sup> A method proposed to restore grasslands uses fences with many small paddocks and moving herds from one paddock to another after a day a two in order to mimick natural grazers and allowing the grass to grow optimally.<sup id="cite_ref-Tlovell_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tlovell-180">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> Additionally, when part of leaf matter is consumed by a herding animal, a corresponding amount of root matter is sloughed off too as it would not be able to sustain the previous amount of root matter and while most of the lost root matter would rot and enter the atmosphere, part of the carbon is sequestered into the soil.<sup id="cite_ref-Tlovell_180-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tlovell-180">[180]</a></sup> It is estimated that increasing the carbon content of the soils in the world’s 3.5 billion hectares of agricultural grassland by 1% would offset nearly 12 years of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-Tlovell_180-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tlovell-180">[180]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Allan_Savory" title="Allan Savory">Allan Savory</a>, as part of <a href="/wiki/Holistic_management" class="mw-redirect" title="Holistic management">holistic management</a>, claims that while large herds are often blamed for desertification, prehistoric lands supported large or larger herds and areas where herds were removed in the United States are still desertifying.<sup id="cite_ref-ASavory_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ASavory-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_capture_and_storage">Carbon capture and storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Carbon capture and storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg/350px-Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg.png" width="350" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg/525px-Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg/700px-Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="530" data-file-height="420" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Schematic showing both terrestrial and geological sequestration of carbon dioxide emissions from a coal-fired plant.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">Carbon capture and storage</a></div>
<p>Carbon capture and storage (CCS) is a method to mitigate climate change by capturing <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) from large point sources such as power plants and subsequently storing it away safely instead of releasing it into the atmosphere. The Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change says CCS could contribute between 10% and 55% of the cumulative worldwide carbon-mitigation effort over the next 90 years. The International Energy Agency says CCS is "the most important single new technology for CO<sub>2</sub> savings" in power generation and industry.<sup id="cite_ref-futureenergysummit_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-futureenergysummit-183">[183]</a></sup> Though it requires up to 40% more energy to run a CCS coal power plant than a regular coal plant, CCS could potentially capture about 90% of all the carbon emitted by the plant.<sup id="cite_ref-futureenergysummit_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-futureenergysummit-183">[183]</a></sup> Norway, which first began storing CO<sub>2</sub>, has cut its emissions by almost a million tons a year, or about 3% of the country's 1990 levels.<sup id="cite_ref-futureenergysummit_183-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-futureenergysummit-183">[183]</a></sup> As of late 2011, the total CO<sub>2</sub> storage capacity of all 14 projects in operation or under construction is over 33 million tonnes a year. This is broadly equivalent to preventing the emissions from more than six million cars from entering the atmosphere each year.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Negative_carbon_dioxide_emissions">Negative carbon dioxide emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Negative carbon dioxide emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Negative_carbon_dioxide_emission" title="Negative carbon dioxide emission">Negative carbon dioxide emission</a></div>
<p>Creating <a href="/wiki/Negative_carbon_dioxide_emission" title="Negative carbon dioxide emission">negative carbon dioxide emissions</a> literally removes carbon from the atmosphere. Examples are <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">direct air capture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biochar" title="Biochar">biochar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bio-energy_with_carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Bio-energy with carbon capture and storage">bio-energy with carbon capture and storage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_weathering" title="Enhanced weathering">enhanced weathering</a> technologies. These processes are sometimes considered as variations of sinks or mitigation,<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IEA_Technology_Roadmap_Carbon_Capture_and_Storage_2009_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA_Technology_Roadmap_Carbon_Capture_and_Storage_2009-186">[186]</a></sup> and sometimes as geoengineering.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>In combination with other mitigation measures, sinks in combination with negative carbon emissions are considered crucial for meeting the <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation_scenarios#350_ppm" title="Climate change mitigation scenarios">350 ppm target</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-hare_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hare-188">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Azar_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Azar-189">[189]</a></sup> and even the less conservative <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation_scenarios#450_ppm" title="Climate change mitigation scenarios">450 ppm target</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geoengineering">Geoengineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Geoengineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">climate engineering</a></div>
<p>Geoengineering is seen by some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2010)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> as an alternative to mitigation and adaptation, but by others<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2010)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> as an entirely separate response to climate change. In a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_literature" title="Scientific literature">literature</a> assessment, Barker <i>et al.</i> (2007) described geoengineering as a type of mitigation policy.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> IPCC (2007) concluded that geoengineering options, such as ocean fertilization to remove CO<sub>2</sub> from the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, remained largely unproven.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> It was judged that reliable cost estimates for geoengineering had not yet been published.</p>
<p>Chapter 28 of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a> report <i>Policy Implications of Greenhouse Warming: Mitigation, Adaptation, and the Science Base</i> (1992) defined geoengineering as "options that would involve large-scale engineering of our environment in order to combat or counteract the effects of changes in atmospheric chemistry."<sup id="cite_ref-nas_policy_implications_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nas_policy_implications-193">[193]</a></sup> They evaluated a range of options to try to give preliminary answers to two questions: can these options work and could they be carried out with a reasonable cost. They also sought to encourage discussion of a third question&#160;— what adverse side effects might there be. The following types of option were examined: reforestation, increasing ocean absorption of carbon dioxide (carbon sequestration) and screening out some sunlight. NAS also argued "Engineered countermeasures need to be evaluated but should not be implemented without broad understanding of the direct effects and the potential side effects, the ethical issues, and the risks.".<sup id="cite_ref-nas_policy_implications_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nas_policy_implications-193">[193]</a></sup> In July 2011 a report by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Government_Accountability_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Government Accountability Office">United States Government Accountability Office</a> on geoengineering found that "[c]limate engineering technologies do not now offer a viable response to global climate change."<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_dioxide_removal">Carbon dioxide removal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Carbon dioxide removal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_removal" title="Carbon dioxide removal">Carbon dioxide removal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_remediation" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenhouse gas remediation">Greenhouse gas remediation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sequestration" title="Carbon sequestration">Carbon sequestration</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_air_capture" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon air capture">Carbon air capture</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_removal" title="Carbon dioxide removal">Carbon dioxide removal</a> has been proposed as a method of reducing the amount of radiative forcing. A variety of means of artificially capturing and storing carbon, as well as of enhancing natural sequestration processes, are being explored. The main natural process is <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> by plants and single-celled organisms (see <a href="/wiki/Biosequestration" title="Biosequestration">biosequestration</a>). Artificial processes vary, and concerns have been expressed about the long-term effects of some of these processes.<sup id="cite_ref-RoyalSociety_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RoyalSociety-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is notable that the availability of cheap energy and appropriate sites for <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">geological storage</a> of carbon may make <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_air_capture" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dioxide air capture">carbon dioxide air capture</a> viable commercially. It is, however, generally expected that carbon dioxide air capture may be uneconomic when compared to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> from major sources&#160;— in particular, fossil fuel powered power stations, refineries, etc. In such cases, costs of energy produced will grow significantly.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, captured CO<sub>2</sub> can be used to force more <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a> out of <a href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil fields</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Statoil" title="Statoil">Statoil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">Shell</a> have made plans to do.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> CO<sub>2</sub> can also be used in commercial <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse" title="Greenhouse">greenhouses</a>, giving an opportunity to kick-start the technology. Some attempts have been made to use <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> to capture <a href="/wiki/Smokestack" class="mw-redirect" title="Smokestack">smokestack</a> emissions,<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> notably the <a href="/wiki/GreenFuel_Technologies_Corporation" title="GreenFuel Technologies Corporation">GreenFuel Technologies Corporation</a>, who have now shut down operations.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_radiation_management">Solar radiation management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Solar radiation management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_radiation_management" title="Solar radiation management">Solar radiation management</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Stratospheric_sulfate_aerosols_(geoengineering)" title="Stratospheric sulfate aerosols (geoengineering)">Stratospheric sulfate aerosols (geoengineering)</a></div>
<p>The main purpose of solar radiation management seek to reflect sunlight and thus reduce global warming. The ability of stratospheric <a href="/wiki/Sulfate_aerosols" class="mw-redirect" title="Sulfate aerosols">sulfate aerosols</a> to create a <a href="/wiki/Global_dimming" title="Global dimming">global dimming</a> effect has made them a possible candidate for use in <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">climate engineering</a> projects.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-CO2_greenhouse_gases">Non-CO<sub>2</sub> greenhouse gases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Non-CO2 greenhouse gases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>CO<sub>2</sub> is not the only GHG relevant to mitigation,<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> and governments have acted to regulate the emissions of other GHGs emitted by human activities (<a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a> GHGs). The emissions caps agreed to by most developed countries under the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a> regulate the emissions of almost all the anthropogenic GHGs.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> These gases are CO<sub>2</sub>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (CH<sub>4</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (N<sub>2</sub>O), the <a href="/wiki/Halocarbon" title="Halocarbon">hydrofluorocarbons</a> (HFC), perfluorocarbons (PFC), and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" title="Sulfur hexafluoride">sulfur hexafluoride</a> (SF<sub>6</sub>).</p>
<p>Stabilizing the atmospheric concentrations of the different anthropogenic GHGs requires an understanding of their different physical properties. Stabilization depends both on how quickly GHGs are added to the atmosphere and how fast they are removed. The rate of removal is measured by the atmospheric lifetime of the GHG in question (see the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Global_warming_potential" title="Greenhouse gas">main GHG article</a> for a list). Here, the lifetime is defined as the time required for a given perturbation of the GHG in the atmosphere to be reduced to 37% of its initial amount.<sup id="cite_ref-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations-19">[19]</a></sup> Methane has a relatively short atmospheric lifetime of about 12 years, while N<sub>2</sub>O's lifetime is about 110 years.
