For methane, a reduction of about 30% below current emission levels would lead to a stabilization in its atmospheric concentration, while for N<sub>2</sub>O, an emissions reduction of more than 50% would be required.<sup id="cite_ref-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations_19-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> is a significantly more potent greenhouse gas than <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> in the amount of heat it can trap, especially in the short term.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> Burning one molecule of methane generates one molecule of carbon dioxide, indicating there may be no net benefit in using gas as a fuel source.<sup id="cite_ref-usclimatenetwork.org_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usclimatenetwork.org-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-epa.gov_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa.gov-135">[135]</a></sup> Reducing the amount of waste methane produced in the first place and moving away from use of gas as a fuel source will have a greater beneficial impact, as might other approaches to productive use of otherwise-wasted methane. In terms of prevention, vaccines are being developed in Australia to reduce the significant global warming contributions from <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> released by livestock via <a href="/wiki/Flatulence" title="Flatulence">flatulence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eructation" class="mw-redirect" title="Eructation">eructation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another physical property of the anthropogenic GHGs relevant to mitigation is the different abilities of the gases to trap heat (in the form of <a href="/wiki/Infrared_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Infrared radiation">infrared radiation</a>). Some gases are more effective at trapping heat than others, e.g., SF<sub>6</sub> is 22,200 times more effective a GHG than CO<sub>2</sub> on a per-kilogram basis.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> A measure for this physical property is the <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a> (GWP), and is used in the Kyoto Protocol.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although not designed for this purpose, the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a> has probably benefited climate change mitigation efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> The Montreal Protocol is an international <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaty</a> that has successfully reduced emissions of <a href="/wiki/Ozone-depleting_substance" class="mw-redirect" title="Ozone-depleting substance">ozone-depleting substances</a> (for example, <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">CFCs</a>), which are also greenhouse gases.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By_sector">By sector</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: By sector">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TeslaRoadster-front.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/TeslaRoadster-front.jpg/220px-TeslaRoadster-front.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/TeslaRoadster-front.jpg/330px-TeslaRoadster-front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/TeslaRoadster-front.jpg/440px-TeslaRoadster-front.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TeslaRoadster-front.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Roadster" title="Tesla Roadster">Tesla Roadster</a> emits no tailpipe emissions, uses <a href="/wiki/Li-Ion" class="mw-redirect" title="Li-Ion">lithium ion</a> batteries to achieve 220&#160;mi (350&#160;km) per charge, while also capable of going 0–60 in under 4 seconds.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg/220px-BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg/330px-BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg/440px-BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="544" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bicycle" title="Bicycle">Bicycles</a> have almost no <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a> compared to cars, and canal transport may represent a positive option for certain types of freight in the 21st century<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_transport" title="Sustainable transport">Sustainable transport</a></div>
<p>Transportation emissions account for roughly 1/4 of emissions worldwide, and are even more important in terms of impact in developed nations especially in North America and Australia. Many citizens of countries like the United States and Canada who drive personal cars often, see well over half of their climate change impact stemming from the emissions produced from their cars. Modes of mass transportation such as bus, light rail (metro, subway, etc.), and long-distance rail are far and away the most energy-efficient means of motorized transportation for passengers, able to use in many cases over twenty times less energy per person-distance than a personal automobile. Modern <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy-efficient technologies</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrid</a> electric vehicles and <a href="/wiki/Carbon-neutral_fuel" title="Carbon-neutral fuel">carbon-neutral synthetic gasoline &amp; Jet fuel</a> may also help to reduce the consumption of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, land use changes and emissions of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>. Utilizing <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport" title="Rail transport">rail transport</a>, especially electric rail, over the far less efficient <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_aviation" title="Environmental impact of aviation">air transport</a> and <a href="/wiki/Road_transport" title="Road transport">truck transport</a> significantly reduces emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> With the use of electric trains and cars in transportation there is the opportunity to run them with <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_power" title="Low-carbon power">low-carbon power</a>, producing far fewer emissions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_planning">Urban planning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Urban planning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">Urban planning</a></div>
<p>Effective <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> to reduce <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">sprawl</a> aims to decrease Vehicle Miles Travelled (VMT), lowering emissions from transportation. Personal cars are extremely inefficient at moving passengers, while <a href="/wiki/Public_transport" title="Public transport">public transport</a> and bicycles are many times more efficient (as is the simplest form of human transportation, walking). All of these are encouraged by urban/community planning and are an effective way to reduce greenhouse gas emissions. Between 1982 and 1997, the amount of land consumed for <a href="/wiki/Urban_development" class="mw-redirect" title="Urban development">urban development</a> in the United States increased by 47 percent while the nation's population grew by only 17 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> Inefficient <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land use</a> development practices have increased infrastructure costs as well as the amount of energy needed for transportation, community services, and buildings.</p>
<p>At the same time, a growing number of citizens and government officials have begun advocating a smarter approach to land use planning. These <a href="/wiki/Smart_growth" title="Smart growth">smart growth</a> practices include compact community development, multiple transportation choices, mixed land uses, and practices to conserve green space. These programs offer environmental, economic, and quality-of-life benefits; and they also serve to reduce energy usage and greenhouse gas emissions.</p>
<p>Approaches such as <a href="/wiki/New_Urbanism" title="New Urbanism">New Urbanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transit-oriented_development" title="Transit-oriented development">Transit-oriented development</a> seek to reduce distances travelled, especially by private vehicles, encourage <a href="/wiki/Public_transit" class="mw-redirect" title="Public transit">public transit</a> and make <a href="/wiki/Walking" title="Walking">walking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cycling" title="Cycling">cycling</a> more attractive options. This is achieved through "medium-density", <a href="/wiki/Mixed-use_development" title="Mixed-use development">mixed-use planning</a> and the concentration of housing within walking distance of <a href="/wiki/Town_center" class="mw-redirect" title="Town center">town centers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transport_node" class="mw-redirect" title="Transport node">transport nodes</a>.</p>
<p>Smarter growth land use policies have both a direct and indirect effect on energy consuming behavior. For example, transportation energy usage, the number one user of petroleum fuels, could be significantly reduced through more compact and mixed use land development patterns, which in turn could be served by a greater variety of non-automotive based transportation choices.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Building_design">Building design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Building design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_architecture" title="Sustainable architecture">Sustainable architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_building" title="Green building">Green building</a></div>
<p>Emissions from <a href="/wiki/House" title="House">housing</a> are substantial,<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> and government-supported energy efficiency programmes can make a difference.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p>For institutions of higher learning in the United States, greenhouse gas emissions depend primarily on total area of buildings and secondarily on climate.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> If climate is not taken into account, annual greenhouse gas emissions due to energy consumed on campuses plus purchased electricity can be estimated with the formula, <i>E=aS</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>, where <i>a</i> =0.001621 metric tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> equivalent/square foot or 0.0241 metric tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> equivalent/square meter and <i>b</i> = 1.1354.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup></p>
<p>New buildings can be constructed using <a href="/wiki/Passive_solar_building_design" title="Passive solar building design">passive solar building design</a>, <a href="/wiki/Low-energy_building" class="mw-redirect" title="Low-energy building">low-energy building</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Zero-energy_building" title="Zero-energy building">zero-energy building</a> techniques, using <a href="/wiki/Renewable_heat" title="Renewable heat">renewable heat</a> sources. Existing buildings can be made more efficient through the use of insulation, high-efficiency appliances (particularly <a href="/wiki/Hot_water_heater" class="mw-redirect" title="Hot water heater">hot water heaters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Furnaces" class="mw-redirect" title="Furnaces">furnaces</a>), <a href="/wiki/Insulated_glazing" title="Insulated glazing">double- or triple-glazed gas-filled windows</a>, external window shades, and building orientation and siting. Renewable heat sources such as <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_heat_pump" title="Geothermal heat pump">shallow geothermal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Passive_solar" class="mw-redirect" title="Passive solar">passive solar</a> energy reduce the amount of greenhouse gasses emitted. In addition to designing buildings which are more energy-efficient to heat, it is possible to design buildings that are more energy-efficient to cool by using lighter-coloured, more reflective materials in the development of urban areas (e.g. by painting roofs white) and planting trees.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> This saves energy because it cools buildings and reduces the <a href="/wiki/Urban_heat_island" title="Urban heat island">urban heat island</a> effect thus reducing the use of air conditioning.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_agriculture" title="Climate change and agriculture">Climate change and agriculture</a></div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/EPA" class="mw-redirect" title="EPA">EPA</a>, agricultural <a href="/wiki/Soil_management" title="Soil management">soil management</a> practices can lead to production and emission of <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (N2O), a major <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> and air pollutant. Activities that can contribute to <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span> emissions include <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a> usage, <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">tillage</a>. The management of soils accounts for over half of the emissions from the Agriculture sector. Cattle livestocks account for one third of emissions, through methane emissions. Manure management and rice cultivation also produce gaseous emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>Methods that significantly enhance carbon sequestration in soil include <a href="/wiki/No-till_farming" title="No-till farming">no-till farming</a>, residue mulching, <a href="/wiki/Cover_crop" title="Cover crop">cover cropping</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a>, all of which are more widely used in <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic farming</a> than in conventional farming.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> Because only 5% of US farmland currently uses no-till and residue mulching, there is a large potential for carbon sequestration.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2015 study found that farming can deplete soil carbon and render soil incapable of supporting life. Instead the study showed that <a href="/wiki/Conservation_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservation farming">conservation farming</a> can protect carbon in soils, and repair damage over time.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup></p>
<p>The farming practise of <a href="/wiki/Cover_crop" title="Cover crop">cover crops</a> has been recognized as climate-smart agriculture by the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Societal_controls">Societal controls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Societal controls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Personal_carbon_credits" title="Personal carbon credits">Personal carbon credits</a></div>
<p>Another method being examined is to make carbon a new currency by introducing tradeable "<a href="/wiki/Personal_carbon_credits" title="Personal carbon credits">personal carbon credits</a>". The idea being it will encourage and motivate individuals to reduce their 'carbon footprint' by the way they live. Each citizen will receive a free annual quota of carbon that they can use to travel, buy food, and go about their business. It has been suggested that by using this concept it could actually solve two problems; pollution and poverty, old age pensioners will actually be better off because they fly less often, so they can cash in their quota at the end of the year to pay heating bills and so forth.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="previous source deadlink (April 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Countries_by_Population_Density_in_2015.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Countries_by_Population_Density_in_2015.svg/400px-Countries_by_Population_Density_in_2015.svg.png" width="400" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Countries_by_Population_Density_in_2015.svg/600px-Countries_by_Population_Density_in_2015.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Countries_by_Population_Density_in_2015.svg/800px-Countries_by_Population_Density_in_2015.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="863" data-file-height="396" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Countries_by_Population_Density_in_2015.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">Population density</a> by country</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Various organizations promote <a href="/wiki/Population_control" title="Population control">population control</a> as a means for mitigating global warming.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> Proposed measures include improving access to <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">family planning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">reproductive health</a> care and information, reducing <a href="/wiki/Natalistic_politics" class="mw-redirect" title="Natalistic politics">natalistic politics</a>, public education about the consequences of continued population growth, and improving access of women to education and economic opportunities.</p>
<p>Population control efforts are impeded by there being somewhat of a taboo in some countries against considering any such efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> Also, various religions <a href="/wiki/Religious_views_on_birth_control" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious views on birth control">discourage or prohibit</a> some or all forms of <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a>.</p>
<p>Population size has a different per capita effect on global warming in different countries, since the per capita production of anthropogenic greenhouse gases varies greatly by country.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Costs_and_benefits">Costs and benefits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Costs and benefits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_climate_change_mitigation" title="Economics of climate change mitigation">Economics of climate change mitigation</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Costs">Costs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Costs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Stern_Review" title="Stern Review">Stern Review</a> proposes stabilising the concentration of greenhouse-gas emissions in the atmosphere at a maximum of 550ppm CO<sub>2</sub>e by 2050. The Review estimates that this would mean cutting total greenhouse-gas emissions to three quarters of 2007 levels. The Review further estimates that the cost of these cuts would be in the range −1.0 to +3.5% of World <a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a>, (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Gross_world_product" title="Gross world product">GWP</a>), with an average estimate of approximately 1%.<sup id="cite_ref-stern_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stern-171">[171]</a></sup> Stern has since revised his estimate to 2% of GWP.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> For comparison, the Gross World Product (GWP) at <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">PPP</a> was estimated at $74.5 trillion in 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> thus 2% is approximately $1.5 trillion. The Review emphasises that these costs are contingent on steady reductions in the cost of low-carbon technologies. Mitigation costs will also vary according to how and when emissions are cut: early, well-planned action will minimise the costs.<sup id="cite_ref-stern_171-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stern-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>One way of estimating the cost of reducing emissions is by considering the likely costs of potential technological and output changes. Policy makers can compare the <a href="/wiki/Marginal_abatement_costs" class="mw-redirect" title="Marginal abatement costs">marginal abatement costs</a> of different methods to assess the cost and amount of possible abatement over time. The marginal abatement costs of the various measures will differ by country, by sector, and over time.<sup id="cite_ref-stern_171-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stern-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Benefits">Benefits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Benefits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Extreme_weather_in_the_US_1980-2011.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Extreme_weather_in_the_US_1980-2011.png/350px-Extreme_weather_in_the_US_1980-2011.png" width="350" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Extreme_weather_in_the_US_1980-2011.png/525px-Extreme_weather_in_the_US_1980-2011.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Extreme_weather_in_the_US_1980-2011.png/700px-Extreme_weather_in_the_US_1980-2011.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Extreme_weather_in_the_US_1980-2011.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Total extreme weather cost and number of events costing more than $1 billion in the United States from 1980 to 2011.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Yohe <i>et al.</i> (2007) assessed the literature on sustainability and climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> With high confidence, they suggested that up to the year 2050, an effort to cap greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions at 550&#160;ppm would benefit developing countries significantly. This was judged to be especially the case when combined with enhanced adaptation. By 2100, however, it was still judged likely that there would be significant <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">effects of global warming</a>. This was judged to be the case even with aggressive mitigation and significantly enhanced <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_capacity" title="Adaptive capacity">adaptive capacity</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sharing">Sharing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Sharing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One of the aspects of mitigation is how to share the costs and benefits of mitigation policies. There is no scientific consensus over how to share these costs and benefits (Toth <i>et al.</i>, 2001).<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> In terms of the politics of mitigation, the UNFCCC's ultimate objective is to stabilize concentrations of GHG in the atmosphere at a level that would <a href="/wiki/Avoiding_dangerous_climate_change" class="mw-redirect" title="Avoiding dangerous climate change">prevent "dangerous" climate change</a> (Rogner <i>et al.</i>, 2007).<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p>GHG emissions are an important correlate of wealth, at least at present (Banuri <i>et al.</i>, 1996, pp.&#160;91–92).<sup id="cite_ref-banuri_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-banuri-235">[235]</a></sup> Wealth, as measured by per capita income (i.e., income per head of population), varies widely between different countries. Activities of the poor that involve emissions of GHGs are often associated with basic needs, such as <a href="/wiki/Heating" class="mw-redirect" title="Heating">heating</a> to stay tolerably warm. In richer countries, emissions tend to be associated with things like <a href="/wiki/Car" title="Car">cars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_heating" title="Central heating">central heating</a>, etc. The impacts of cutting emissions could therefore have different impacts on human <a href="/wiki/Welfare_economics" title="Welfare economics">welfare</a> according to wealth.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Distributing_emissions_abatement_costs">Distributing emissions abatement costs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Distributing emissions abatement costs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There have been different proposals on how to allocate responsibility for cutting emissions (Banuri <i>et al.</i>, 1996, pp.&#160;103–105):<sup id="cite_ref-banuri_235-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-banuri-235">[235]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">Egalitarianism</a></b>: this system interprets the problem as one where each person has equal rights to a global resource, i.e., polluting the atmosphere.</li>
<li><b>Basic needs</b>: this system would have emissions allocated according to basic needs, as defined according to a minimum level of <a href="/wiki/Consumption_(economics)" title="Consumption (economics)">consumption</a>. Consumption above basic needs would require countries to buy more emission rights. From this viewpoint, developing countries would need to be at least as well off under an emissions control regime as they would be outside the regime.</li>
<li><b>Proportionality and polluter-pays principle</b>: Proportionality reflects the ancient <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian</a> principle that people should receive in proportion to what they put in, and pay in proportion to the damages they cause. This has a potential relationship with the "polluter-pays principle", which can be interpreted in a number of ways:
<ul>
<li><i>Historical responsibilities</i>: this asserts that allocation of emission rights should be based on patterns of past emissions. Two-thirds of the stock of GHGs in the atmosphere at present is due to the past actions of developed countries (Goldemberg <i>et al.</i>, 1996, p.&#160;29).<sup id="cite_ref-goldemberg_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goldemberg-236">[236]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Comparable burdens and ability to pay</i>: with this approach, countries would reduce emissions based on comparable burdens and their ability to take on the costs of reduction. Ways to assess burdens include monetary costs per head of population, as well as other, more complex measures, like the <a href="/wiki/UNDP" class="mw-redirect" title="UNDP">UNDP</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Willingness_to_pay" title="Willingness to pay">Willingness to pay</a></i>: with this approach, countries take on emission reductions based on their ability to pay along with how much they benefit from reducing their emissions.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Specific_proposals">Specific proposals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Specific proposals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li><b>Ad hoc</b>: Lashof (1992) and Cline (1992) (referred to by Banuri <i>et al.</i>, 1996, p.&#160;106),<sup id="cite_ref-banuri_235-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-banuri-235">[235]</a></sup> for example, suggested that allocations based partly on <a href="/wiki/GNP" class="mw-redirect" title="GNP">GNP</a> could be a way of sharing the burdens of emission reductions. This is because GNP and economic activity are partially tied to carbon emissions.</li>
<li><b>Equal per capita entitlements</b>: this is the most widely cited method of distributing abatement costs, and is derived from egalitarianism (Banuri <i>et al.</i>, 1996, pp.&#160;106–107). This approach can be divided into two categories. In the first category, emissions are allocated according to national population. In the second category, emissions are allocated in a way that attempts to account for historical (cumulative) emissions.</li>
<li><b>Status quo</b>: with this approach, historical emissions are ignored, and current emission levels are taken as a status quo right to emit (Banuri <i>et al.</i>, 1996, p.&#160;107). An analogy for this approach can be made with <a href="/wiki/Fishery" title="Fishery">fisheries</a>, which is a common, limited resource. The analogy would be with the atmosphere, which can be viewed as an exhaustible <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resource</a> (Goldemberg <i>et al.</i>, 1996, p.&#160;27).<sup id="cite_ref-goldemberg_236-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goldemberg-236">[236]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a>, one state recognized the long-established use of another state's use of the fisheries resource. It was also recognized by the state that part of the other state's economy was dependent on that resource.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governmental_and_intergovernmental_action">Governmental and intergovernmental action</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Governmental and intergovernmental action">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_global_warming" title="Politics of global warming">Politics of global warming</a></div>
<table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">Bringing down emissions of greenhouse gases asks a good deal of people, not least that they accept the science of climate change. It requires them to make sacrifices today so that future generations will suffer less, and to weigh the needs of people who are living far away.</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>—&#160;<i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i>, 28 November 2015<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Many countries, both developing and developed, are aiming to use cleaner technologies (World Bank, 2010, p.&#160;192).<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> Use of these technologies aids mitigation and could result in substantial reductions in CO<sub>2</sub> emissions. Policies include targets for emissions reductions, increased use of renewable energy, and increased energy efficiency. It is often argued that the results of climate change are more damaging in poor nations, where infrastructures are weak and few social services exist. The <a href="/wiki/Commitment_to_Development_Index" title="Commitment to Development Index">Commitment to Development Index</a> is one attempt to analyze rich country policies taken to reduce their disproportionate use of the global commons. Countries do well if their greenhouse gas emissions are falling, if their gas taxes are high, if they do not subsidize the fishing industry, if they have a low fossil fuel rate per capita, and if they control imports of illegally cut tropical timber.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kyoto_Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Kyoto Protocol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a></div>
<p>The main current international agreement on combating climate change is the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a>, which came into force on 16 February 2005. The Kyoto Protocol is an <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Amendment" class="extiw" title="wikt:Amendment">amendment</a> to the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC). <a href="/wiki/List_of_Kyoto_Protocol_signatories" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Kyoto Protocol signatories">Countries that have ratified this protocol</a> have committed to reduce their emissions of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and five other greenhouse gases, or engage in <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">emissions trading</a> if they maintain or increase emissions of these gases.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature_targets">Temperature targets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Temperature targets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_(PBL)EN.svg" class="image"><img alt="Refer to caption and image description" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_%28PBL%29EN.svg/400px-Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_%28PBL%29EN.svg.png" width="400" height="416" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_%28PBL%29EN.svg/600px-Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_%28PBL%29EN.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_%28PBL%29EN.svg/800px-Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_%28PBL%29EN.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="980" data-file-height="1020" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_(PBL)EN.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The graph on the right shows three "pathways" to meet the UNFCCC's 2&#160;°C target, labelled "global technology", "decentralised solutions", and "consumption change". Each pathway shows how various measures (e.g., improved energy efficiency, increased use of renewable energy) could contribute to emissions reductions. Image credit: PBL <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_Environmental_Assessment_Agency" title="Netherlands Environmental Assessment Agency">Netherlands Environmental Assessment Agency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Actions to mitigate climate change are sometimes based on the goal of achieving a particular temperature target. One of the targets that has been suggested is to limit the future increase in global mean temperature (global warming) to below 2&#160;°C, relative to the pre-industrial level.<sup id="cite_ref-Jaeger.2C_C.C..2C_and_J._Jaeger_2011_15.E2.80.9326_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jaeger.2C_C.C..2C_and_J._Jaeger_2011_15.E2.80.9326-240">[240]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stockholm_Environment_Institute_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stockholm_Environment_Institute-241">[241]</a></sup> The 2&#160;°C target was adopted in 2010 by Parties to the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change.<sup id="cite_ref-UNFCCC_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNFCCC-242">[242]</a></sup> Most countries of the world are Parties to the UNFCCC.<sup id="cite_ref-unfccc.int_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfccc.int-243">[243]</a></sup> The target had been adopted in 1996 by the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Council of the European Union">European Union Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Feasibility of 2&#160;°C</dt>
</dl>
<p>Temperatures have increased by 0.8&#160;°C compared to the pre-industrial level, and another 0.5–0.7&#160;°C is already <a href="/wiki/Climate_commitment" title="Climate commitment">committed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> The 2&#160;°C rise is typically associated in <a href="/wiki/Climate_model" title="Climate model">climate models</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">carbon dioxide equivalent</a> concentration of 400–500 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> by volume; the current (January 2015) level of carbon dioxide alone is 400 ppm by volume, and rising at 1–3 ppm annually. Hence, to avoid a very likely breach of the 2&#160;°C target, CO<sub>2</sub> levels would have to be stabilised very soon; this is generally regarded as unlikely, based on current programs in place to date.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> The importance of change is illustrated by the fact that world economic energy efficiency is improving at only half the rate of world <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Views in the literature</dt>
</dl>
<p>There is disagreement among experts over whether or not the 2&#160;°C target can be met.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> For example, according to Anderson and Bows (2011),<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> "there is little to no chance" of meeting the target. On the other hand, according to Alcamo <i>et al.</i> (2013):<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Policies adopted by parties to the UNFCCC are too weak to meet a 2 or 1.5&#160;°C target. However, these targets might still be achievable if more stringent mitigation policies are adopted immediately.</li>
<li>Cost-effective 2&#160;°C scenarios project annual global greenhouse gas emissions to peak before the year 2020, with deep cuts in emissions thereafter, leading to a reduction in 2050 of 41% compared to 1990 levels.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>Discussion on other targets</dt>
</dl>
<p>Scientific analysis can provide information on the impacts of climate change and associated policies, such as reducing GHG emissions. However, deciding what policies are best requires value judgements.<sup id="cite_ref-wg_2_ch19_dangerous_climate_change_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wg_2_ch19_dangerous_climate_change-12">[12]</a></sup> For example, limiting global warming to 1&#160;°C relative to pre-industrial levels may help to reduce climate change damages more than a 2&#160;°C limit.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> However, a 1&#160;°C limit may be more costly to achieve than a 2&#160;°C limit.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to some analysts, the 2&#160;°C "guardrail" is inadequate for the needed degree and timeliness of mitigation.<sup id="cite_ref-existing_policies_inadequate_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-existing_policies_inadequate-16">[16]</a></sup> On the other hand, some economic studies suggest more modest mitigation policies.<sup id="cite_ref-van_vuuren_mitigation_policy_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-van_vuuren_mitigation_policy-17">[17]</a></sup> For example, the emissions reductions proposed by Nordhaus (2010)<sup id="cite_ref-nordhaus_2010_cba_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nordhaus_2010_cba-18">[18]</a></sup> might lead to global warming (in the year 2100) of around 3&#160;°C, relative to pre-industrial levels.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Official long-term target of 1.5&#160;°C</dt>
</dl>
<p>In 2015, two official UNFCCC scientific expert bodies came to the conclusion that, "in some regions and vulnerable ecosystems, high risks are projected even for warming above 1.5°C".<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> This expert position was, together with the strong diplomatic voice of the poorest countries and the island nations in the Pacific, the driving force leading to the decision of the <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2015 United Nations Climate Change Conference">Paris Conference</a> 2015, to lay down this 1.5&#160;°C long-term target on top of the existing 2&#160;°C goal.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Encouraging_use_changes">Encouraging use changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Encouraging use changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Emissions_tax">Emissions tax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Emissions tax">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Energy_tax" title="Energy tax">energy tax</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fee_and_dividend" title="Fee and dividend">fee and dividend</a></div>
<p>An emissions tax on greenhouse gas emissions requires individual emitters to pay a fee, charge or tax for every tonne of greenhouse gas released into the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-gupta_emissions_tax_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gupta_emissions_tax-258">[258]</a></sup> Most environmentally related taxes with implications for greenhouse gas emissions in OECD countries are levied on energy products and motor vehicles, rather than on CO<sub>2</sub> emissions directly.<sup id="cite_ref-gupta_emissions_tax_258-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gupta_emissions_tax-258">[258]</a></sup></p>
<p>Emission taxes can be both cost-effective and environmentally effective.<sup id="cite_ref-gupta_emissions_tax_258-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gupta_emissions_tax-258">[258]</a></sup> Difficulties with emission taxes include their potential unpopularity, and the fact that they cannot guarantee a particular level of emissions reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-gupta_emissions_tax_258-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gupta_emissions_tax-258">[258]</a></sup> Emissions or energy taxes also often fall disproportionately on lower income classes. In developing countries, institutions may be insufficiently developed for the collection of emissions fees from a wide variety of sources.<sup id="cite_ref-gupta_emissions_tax_258-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gupta_emissions_tax-258">[258]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsidies">Subsidies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Subsidies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Mark_Z._Jacobson" title="Mark Z. Jacobson">Mark Z. Jacobson</a>, a program of subsidization balanced against expected flood costs could pay for conversion to 100% renewable power by 2030.<sup id="cite_ref-wws-review_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wws-review-259">[259]</a></sup> Jacobson, and his colleague Mark Delucchi, suggest that the cost to generate and transmit power in 2020 will be less than 4 cents per kilowatt hour (in 2007 dollars) for wind, about 4 cents for wave and hydroelectric, from 4 to 7 cents for geothermal, and 8 cents per kWh for solar, fossil, and nuclear power.<sup id="cite_ref-wws_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wws-260">[260]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Investment">Investment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Investment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_divestment" title="Fossil fuel divestment">Fossil fuel divestment</a></div>
<p>Another indirect method of encouraging uses of renewable energy, and pursue sustainability and environmental protection, is that of prompting investment in this area through legal means, something that is already being done at national level as well as in the field of international investment.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_emissions_trading">Carbon emissions trading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Carbon emissions trading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions_trading" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon emissions trading">Carbon emissions trading</a></div>
<p>With the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Market_(economics)" title="Market (economics)">market</a> for <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions_trading" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon emissions trading">trading carbon dioxide emissions</a> within the Kyoto Protocol, it is likely that London financial markets will be the centre for this potentially highly lucrative business; the <a href="/wiki/NYSE" class="mw-redirect" title="NYSE">New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> stock markets may have a lower trade volume than expected as long as the US maintains its rejection of the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, emissions trading may delay the phase-out of fossil fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the north-east United States, a successful cap and trade program has shown potential for this solution.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Emission_Trading_Scheme" title="European Union Emission Trading Scheme">European Union Emission Trading Scheme</a> (EU ETS)<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup> is the largest multi-national, greenhouse gas emissions trading scheme in the world. It commenced operation on 1 January 2005, and all 28 member states of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> participate in the scheme which has created a new market in carbon dioxide allowances estimated at 35&#160;billion Euros (US$43&#160;billion) per year.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Climate_Exchange" title="Chicago Climate Exchange">Chicago Climate Exchange</a> was the first (voluntary) emissions market, and is soon to be followed by Asia's first market (<a href="/w/index.php?title=Asia_Carbon_Exchange&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Asia Carbon Exchange (page does not exist)">Asia Carbon Exchange</a>). A total of 107 million metric tonnes of carbon dioxide equivalent have been exchanged through projects in 2004, a 38% increase relative to 2003 (78 Mt CO<sub>2</sub>e).<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup></p>
<p>Twenty three <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational corporations</a> have come together in the <a href="/wiki/G8_Climate_Change_Roundtable" title="G8 Climate Change Roundtable">G8 Climate Change Roundtable</a>, a business group formed at the January 2005 <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a>. The group includes <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">British Airways</a> and <a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">BP</a>. On 9 June 2005 the Group published a statement<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup> stating that there was a need to act on climate change and claiming that market-based solutions can help. It called on governments to establish "clear, transparent, and consistent price signals" through "creation of a long-term policy framework" that would include all major producers of greenhouse gases.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Regional_Greenhouse_Gas_Initiative" title="Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative">Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative</a> is a proposed carbon trading scheme being created by nine North-eastern and Mid-Atlantic <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> states; <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" class="mw-redirect" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>. The scheme was due to be developed by April 2005 but has not yet been completed.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Implementation">Implementation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Implementation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Implementation puts into effect climate change mitigation strategies and targets. These can be targets set by international bodies or voluntary action by individuals or institutions. This is the most important, expensive and least appealing aspect of environmental governance.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-269">[269]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding">Funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Funding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Implementation requires funding sources but is often beset by disputes over who should provide funds and under what conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_269-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-269">[269]</a></sup> A lack of funding can be a barrier to successful strategies as there are no formal arrangements to finance climate change development and implementation.<sup id="cite_ref-IHDP_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHDP-270">[270]</a></sup> Funding is often provided by nations, groups of nations and increasingly NGO and private sources. These funds are often channelled through the Global Environmental Facility (GEF). This is an environmental funding mechanism in the World Bank which is designed to deal with global environmental issues.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_269-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-269">[269]</a></sup> The GEF was originally designed to tackle four main areas: biological diversity, climate change, international waters and ozone layer depletion, to which land degradation and persistent organic pollutant were added. The GEF funds projects that are agreed to achieve global environmental benefits that are endorsed by governments and screened by one of the GEF’s implementing agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems">Problems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Problems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are numerous issues which result in a current perceived lack of implementation.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_269-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-269">[269]</a></sup> It has been suggested that the main barriers to implementation are, Uncertainty, Fragmentation, Institutional void, Short time horizon of policies and politicians and Missing motives and willingness to start adapting. The relationships between many climatic processes can cause large levels of uncertainty as they are not fully understood and can be a barrier to implementation. When information on climate change is held between the large numbers of actors involved it can be highly dispersed, context specific or difficult to access causing fragmentation to be a barrier. Institutional void is the lack of commonly accepted rules and norms for policy processes to take place, calling into question the legitimacy and efficacy of policy processes. The Short time horizon of policies and politicians often means that climate change policies are not implemented in favour of socially favoured societal issues. Statements are often posed to keep the illusion of political action to prevent or postpone decisions being made. Missing motives and willingness to start adapting is a large barrier as it prevents any implementation.<sup id="cite_ref-IHDP_270-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHDP-270">[270]</a></sup></p>
<p>The issues that arise with a system which involves international government cooperation, such as <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">Cap and Trade</a>, could potentially be improved with a polycentric approach where the rules are enforced by many small sections of authority as apposed to one overall enforcement agency.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Despite a perceived lack of occurrence, evidence of implementation is emerging internationally. Some examples of this are the initiation of NAPA’s and of joint implementation. Many developing nations have made National Adaptation Programs of Action (NAPAs) which are frameworks to prioritize adaption needs.<sup id="cite_ref-EMJ_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EMJ-273">[273]</a></sup> The implementation of many of these is supported by GEF agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup> Many developed countries are implementing ‘first generation’ institutional adaption plans particularly at the state and local government scale.<sup id="cite_ref-EMJ_273-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EMJ-273">[273]</a></sup> There has also been a push towards joint implementation between countries by the UNFCC as this has been suggested as a cost-effective way for objectives to be achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Territorial_policies">Territorial policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Territorial policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions">List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_the_United_States" title="Climate change in the United States">Climate change in the United States</a></div>
<p>Efforts to reduce <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_the_United_States" title="Greenhouse gas emissions by the United States">greenhouse gas emissions by the United States</a> include <a href="/wiki/Energy_policy_of_the_United_States" title="Energy policy of the United States">energy policies</a> which encourage efficiency through programs like <a href="/wiki/Energy_Star" title="Energy Star">Energy Star</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Building_Integration" class="mw-redirect" title="Commercial Building Integration">Commercial Building Integration</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Technologies_Program" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial Technologies Program">Industrial Technologies Program</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup> On 12 November 1998, Vice President <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a> symbolically signed the Kyoto Protocol, but he indicated participation by the developing nations was necessary prior its being submitted for ratification by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, Transportation Secretary <a href="/wiki/Mary_Peters_(politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary Peters (politician)">Mary Peters</a>, with White House approval, urged governors and dozens of members of the House of Representatives to block California’s first-in-the-nation limits on greenhouse gases from cars and trucks, according to e-mails obtained by Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/U.S._Climate_Change_Science_Program" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Climate Change Science Program">U.S. Climate Change Science Program</a> is a group of about twenty federal agencies and US Cabinet Departments, all working together to address global warming.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bush_administration_(2000)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bush administration (2000)">Bush administration</a> pressured American scientists to suppress discussion of global warming, according to the testimony of the Union of Concerned Scientists to the Oversight and Government Reform Committee of the U.S. House of Representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-279">[279]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup> "High-quality science" was "struggling to get out," as the Bush administration pressured scientists to tailor their writings on global warming to fit the Bush administration's skepticism, in some cases at the behest of an ex-oil industry lobbyist. "Nearly half of all respondents perceived or personally experienced pressure to eliminate the words 'climate change,' 'global warming' or other similar terms from a variety of communications." Similarly, according to the testimony of senior officers of the <a href="/wiki/Government_Accountability_Project" title="Government Accountability Project">Government Accountability Project</a>, the White House attempted to bury the report "National Assessment of the Potential Consequences of Climate Variability and Change," produced by U.S. scientists pursuant to U.S. law.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup> Some U.S. scientists resigned their jobs rather than give in to White House pressure to underreport global warming.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_279-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-279">[279]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the absence of substantial federal action, state governments have adopted emissions-control laws such as the <a href="/wiki/Regional_Greenhouse_Gas_Initiative" title="Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative">Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative</a> in the Northeast and the <a href="/wiki/Global_Warming_Solutions_Act_of_2006" title="Global Warming Solutions Act of 2006">Global Warming Solutions Act of 2006</a> in California.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Developing_countries">Developing countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Developing countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In order to reconcile <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a> with mitigating carbon emissions, <a href="/wiki/Developing_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a> need particular support, both financial and technical. One of the means of achieving this is the Kyoto Protocol's <a href="/wiki/Clean_Development_Mechanism" title="Clean Development Mechanism">Clean Development Mechanism</a> (CDM). The <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>'s Prototype Carbon Fund<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Public_private_partnership" class="mw-redirect" title="Public private partnership">public private partnership</a> that operates within the CDM.</p>
<p>An important point of contention, however, is how overseas development assistance not directly related to climate change mitigation is affected by funds provided to climate change mitigation.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-284">[284]</a></sup> One of the outcomes of the UNFCC <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_Climate_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Copenhagen Climate Conference">Copenhagen Climate Conference</a> was the <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_Accord" title="Copenhagen Accord">Copenhagen Accord</a>, in which developed countries promised to provide US $30&#160;million between 2010 and 2012 of new and additional resources.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_284-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-284">[284]</a></sup> Yet it remains unclear what exactly the definition of additional is and the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> has requested its member states to define what they understand to be additional, and researchers at the <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Development_Institute" title="Overseas Development Institute">Overseas Development Institute</a> have found four main understandings:<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_284-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-284">[284]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>Climate finance classified as aid, but additional to (over and above) the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Development_Goals#Controversy_over_funding_of_0.7.25_of_GNI" title="Millennium Development Goals">‘0.7%’ ODA target</a>;</li>
<li>Increase on previous year's <a href="/wiki/Official_Development_Assistance" class="mw-redirect" title="Official Development Assistance">Official Development Assistance</a> (ODA) spent on climate change mitigation;</li>
<li>Rising ODA levels that include climate change finance but where it is limited to a specified percentage; and</li>
<li>Increase in climate finance not connected to ODA.</li>
</ol>
<p>The main point being that there is a conflict between the <a href="/wiki/OECD" class="mw-redirect" title="OECD">OECD</a> states budget deficit cuts, the need to help developing countries adapt to develop sustainably and the need to ensure that funding does not come from cutting aid to other important <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Development_Goals" title="Millennium Development Goals">Millennium Development Goals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_284-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-284">[284]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, none of these initiatives suggest a quantitative cap on the emissions from developing countries. This is considered as a particularly difficult policy proposal as the economic growth of developing countries are proportionally reflected in the growth of greenhouse emissions. Critics<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2010)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> of mitigation often argue that, the developing countries' drive to attain a comparable living standard to the developed countries would doom the attempt at mitigation of global warming. Critics<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2010)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> also argue that holding down emissions would shift the human cost of global warming from a general one to one that was borne most heavily by the poorest populations on the planet.</p>
<p>In an attempt to provide more opportunities for developing countries to adapt clean technologies, <a href="/wiki/UNEP" class="mw-redirect" title="UNEP">UNEP</a> and <a href="/wiki/WTO" class="mw-redirect" title="WTO">WTO</a> urged the international community to reduce trade barriers and to conclude the <a href="/wiki/Doha_round" class="mw-redirect" title="Doha round">Doha trade round</a> "which includes opening trade in environmental goods and services".<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-governmental_approaches">Non-governmental approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Non-governmental approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While many of the proposed methods of mitigating global warming require governmental funding, legislation and regulatory action, individuals and <a href="/wiki/Business_action_on_climate_change" title="Business action on climate change">businesses</a> can also play a part in the mitigation effort.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Choices_in_personal_actions_and_business_operations">Choices in personal actions and business operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Choices in personal actions and business operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Environmental groups encourage <a href="/wiki/Individual_and_political_action_on_climate_change" title="Individual and political action on climate change">individual action against global warming</a>, often aimed at the <a href="/wiki/Consumer" title="Consumer">consumer</a>. Common recommendations include lowering home heating and cooling usage, burning less gasoline, supporting renewable <a href="/wiki/Energy_sources" class="mw-redirect" title="Energy sources">energy sources</a>, buying local products to reduce transportation, turning off unused devices, and various others.</p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Geophysicist" class="mw-redirect" title="Geophysicist">geophysicist</a> at <a href="/wiki/Utrecht_University" title="Utrecht University">Utrecht University</a> has urged similar institutions to hold the vanguard in voluntary mitigation, suggesting the use of communications technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Videoconferencing" title="Videoconferencing">videoconferencing</a> to reduce their dependence on long-haul flights.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_travel_and_shipment">Air travel and shipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Air travel and shipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2008, climate scientist <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Anderson_(scientist)" title="Kevin Anderson (scientist)">Kevin Anderson</a> raised concern about the growing effect of rapidly increasing global air transport on the climate in a paper,<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup> and a presentation,<sup id="cite_ref-anderson_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-288">[288]</a></sup> suggesting that reversing this trend is necessary to reduce emissions.</p>
<p>Part of the difficulty is that when <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_aviation" title="Environmental impact of aviation">aviation emissions</a> are made at high altitude, the climate impacts are much greater than otherwise. Others have been raising the related concerns of the increasing <a href="/wiki/Hypermobility_(travel)" title="Hypermobility (travel)">hypermobility</a> of individuals, whether traveling for business or pleasure, involving frequent and often long distance air travel, as well as air shipment of goods.<sup id="cite_ref-gossling_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gossling-289">[289]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_opportunities_and_risks">Business opportunities and risks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Business opportunities and risks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Business_action_on_climate_change" title="Business action on climate change">Business action on climate change</a></div>
<p>On 9 May 2005 <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Immelt" class="mw-redirect" title="Jeff Immelt">Jeff Immelt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chief_executive" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief executive">chief executive</a> of <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> (GE), announced plans to reduce GE's global warming related emissions by one percent by 2012. "GE said that given its projected growth, those emissions would have risen by 40 percent without such action."<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 21 June 2005 a group of leading <a href="/wiki/Airline" title="Airline">airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Airport" title="Airport">airports</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aerospace" title="Aerospace">aerospace</a> <a href="/wiki/Manufacturer" class="mw-redirect" title="Manufacturer">manufacturers</a> pledged to work together to reduce the negative <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_aviation" title="Environmental impact of aviation">environmental impact of aviation</a>, including limiting the impact of air travel on climate change by improving <a href="/wiki/Fuel_efficiency" title="Fuel efficiency">fuel efficiency</a> and reducing carbon dioxide emissions of new aircraft by fifty percent per seat kilometre by 2020 from 2000 levels. The group aims to develop a common reporting system for carbon dioxide emissions per aircraft by the end of 2005, and pressed for the early inclusion of aviation in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>'s carbon emission trading scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Investor_response">Investor response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Investor response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
